I don't know if anyone is going to read this comment after so much time has passed since this video, but I want to add some fun facts. As Rich said at the beginning of the video, the Turtles' art initially all wore red masks. The colors and the initials on the belt were added by the cartoon to visually individualize each Turtle. The first April in the film was replaced due to production issues. She defended the actors inside the Turtles because they were mistreated and underpaid. The actor who plays Rafael has a cameo as the taxi passenger. The Asian friend in the second one is one of the actors who was inside one of the Turtles in the first one. He made such a good impression with his technique and acting that they created that role for him for the sequel. The Turtles' heads had so many motors inside to control their movements that they overheated, and the actors had to constantly cool themselves.

Fun Fact; the guy in the cab who says, "What the heck was that?!" when Raph rolls over the hood of the cab, is the guy who does Ralph's voice.
As a young teen, I think TMNT resonated because of the themes of father and sons, brotherhood, duty versus freedom, and being an outsider... AND ninjas.
The original TMNT comic was a joke in one way because the idea parodied all the most prominent 80s comic tropes; like mutants (from X-Men and The Dark Knight Returns), teenagers (from Teen Titans and X-Men), seemingly random talking animal characters (like Howard the Duck), and a dark and gritty tone (comics at that time in general). But I honestly don't know if I'd go as far as to call the original comic as a whole a "joke" or a straight-up "parody." Despite the fact that the original idea started out as a joke between friends, the obvious parodic elements of the concept, and the references in the original story to Daredevil, the top priority of Eastman and Laird's original comic seemed to be carving out a unique name for itself in the then-current comic book landscape. And a good way to do that was by hooking consumers with humorously familiar tropes. I mean it can't really be a straight parody if Eastman and Laird wanted to tell those exact kinds of stories in a sincere way. Because honestly the Daredevil references in the original comic read more to me as a homage to a property that the creators enjoy, rather than as a parody of Daredevil. I mean it's not like the TMNT even have anything significant in common with Daredevil story-or-character-wise, other than the very general idea of vigilantes dealing with an enemy ninja clan (which can describe many comic stories). TMNT was Eastman and Laird's attempt at genuinely striking a chord with readers in ways that go beyond the strictly parodic. I always thought of it as a parodical idea being used as the vessel to tell the types of comic stories they've always wanted to.

It was so dumb that parents were worried about the turtles using weapons. So instead they got what they wished for, the weapons were removed, but now they‚Äôre using every day household objects and toys as weapons. Now the kids can learn how to use a Yo-yo to put someone‚Äôs eye out. Congratulations.
Story of the Hero turtle name change was down to the head of the British Board of Film "Classification".   There had been BS stories in the tabloid press concerning kids buying ninja weapons,  and he became terrified to the point that they were effectively banned from appearing in non 18 rated movies.  Once he left the job,  the ban was lifted and a little sanity returned to the BBFC.   Anyway,   while the ban was in place it was wiser to keep the word ninja out of the title of any kids entertainment,  so while the turtles weren't censored directly,   the cooling effect led to the name change.

The original movie came out at a time when I was trying to be more adult because I was about to head off to university, and I was a bit sensitive to the fact comics were still seen as a kids' medium, so I wasn't a fan of the cartoons, or the Archie comics version, or the film given how it didn't quite stick to the mood of the original comics. Now, having watched it with my kids, and as an adult who now finds many modern 'mature' comics to be soulless, pretentious drivel, and many others (no, not all) to be political screeds by people with contempt for the industry or who just cannot write, I've reassessed the film. It is decent enough family entertainment that did its best with the limitations with effects at the time, while being something adults can sit through. Something the sequels failed at.

Now don't get me wrong, I don't think Rise is the worst TV show that could happen. But I'd definitely agree that it wasn't the strongest out of the three, as someone who sporadically watched some of the 2003 (That scene of the Shredder's body picking up his head will always haunt me, even though I know he's an Utrom in that series) and kind of more followed the 2012 series growing up. I don't mind changes to a show (I thought the 3D He-Man series did pretty well respecting the source material), but Rise didn't really feel like a TMNT show; The turtles were all different species (which is INTERESTING, but it doesn't work well for TMNT), the characters felt too much like a blend of each other (everyone's goofy and zany in some way) instead of having unique defining personalities (Hell even Michael Bay's movies handled the personalities better. I loved the visual style of the movie, the turtle designs were effective without looking too weird or out of place, and the casting was great. Nolan North as Raphael was awesome.

Great episode! Although I think you have one thing slightly backwards. As far as I know, Jim Henson was the one who brought in Steve Barron, who'd directed the music videos for Labyrinth (which led to him working on Storyteller). Also, Barron's mother taught at an art school, and was a big influence on his work. ... Beyond that, the Teenage Mutant Ninja Turtles were also a parody of Teen Titans and New Mutants (hence "teenage") and Dave Sim's Cerebus and other funny animal comics at the time. And of course the turtles would meet Cerebus on one of their adventures. (This is back when Sim was the king of indie comics, and not the pariah he'd later become‚Äîback when he was making characters like Charles X. Claremont, and Chris Claremont was returning the favor by creating characters like the demon S'ym.)

The suits are not great, the story is eh, and it bothers me still that they have 1500s Japanese peasants speaking perfect English, but the cinematography is actually really terrific for a dumb cash-in sequel.¬†There are some shots and cuts and transitions that take my breath away, particularly in the beginning of the movie. TMNT 1990 isn‚Äôt just an incredible superhero film but a cautionary tale for young people. A frightening preview of where we are at as a society. Urban decay, crime, broken homes, young people wanting so much to feel loved that they join this evil organization that has no problems preying on their desire to be part of a family. 32 years later, these themes are not only present but even worse as watch young people loot and rob and kill in our streets like in my hometown of Chicago. Here is a sobering reality. People like the Foot Clan and Shredder are in your cities. They may not have masks or sharp objects hanging off them but they are in your neighborhoods, your street corners, your blocks. Ready to corrupt your children. TMNT 1990 is a movie about the power and love of family and how the idea of family can be twisted for evil.

As someone who started with the cartoon then saw the movie, oddly this and so many other "motion picture" adaptations didn't throw me for being different. I am not sure when -- it was before Ninja Turtles, I had simply accepted the logic that when cartoons become "real" they simply become different. So this, Masters of the Universe, even Super Mario Bros, I was just very accepting that things were going to be different. No idea where this came from. But given how terrifying costumed cartoon characters could be in the 80s, I think I and a lot of children like me accepted that sometimes...well, the ways you were different could be worse. Not your fault that you are a terrifyingly ugly shell of what you were in a cartoon -- that is just something that happens. ...Now that I think of it, that little girl not even blinking at the aliens in Killer Clowns from Outer Space suddenly makes a lot of sense.

It is funny how many people think of this as a New York movie when it was filmed in Wilmington, NC. Hence some of those city scenes not getting too far off the ground and staying street level. Wilmington was a really hot place for making movies at the time, and remained that way for a while. Screen Gem Studios maintains facilities there. Wasn't TMNT originally intended as a satire of the war on drugs: Adolescent counterculture potheads vs big pharma?   Take the first episode of the 1987 series. Shredder frames Splinter to look disrespectful and dangerous, then went high tech and infiltrated the financial capital of America. The police are woefully incompetent. They put pressure on the media to keep quiet about the conspiracy, and April is shown as a hero for wanting to get the truth out. She at first blames the turtles even though they literally just saved her because the "expert opinions" label anything from Japan as the ninjas responsible for crime, but she comes around once she gets to know them. They're in the middle of solving a case, when they get distracted and order pizza with unusual flavors, referencing a well-known effect of THC. The first time an old lady sees the turtles, she pulls out a machine gun and starts shooting at them, referencing how mainstream society overreacts, and that the opioids being prescribed to the elderly are more dangerous than the ninja turtle weapons.  Shredder replaces real ninjas with cheap synthetic counterfeits. Aside from specifics, the overall vibe feels very much like it was born in a haze-filled room...   I get it was also a parody of other comics, but there's no way this level of reference was unintentional. It's about as subtle as Scooby Dooby Do. Of course, for marketing purposes, admitting a kid show was anti-pharma/pro-cannabis propaganda during the 80s would be financial suicide.

I believe the reason they turned it from 'Ninja Turtles' to 'Hero Turtles' and toned down on the weapons, violence, etc is largely because of the Thatcher Conservative government in the UK. Thatcher's policies led to increased poverty and then a spike in crime, which Tories blamed on so called 'video nasties'. Video nasties was film and TV content deemed too violent, supposedly causing violence in society. After they were banned films like The Exorcist and Texas Chainsaw Massacre didn't even get shown on TV in the UK until the 90s. Because the UK was such a big market for the show it ended up being changed.

Just wanted to point out that while you guys thought Shredder coming back in the was because he was devoured by worms I totally bought into this and here‚Äôs why. Unsurprisingly I am a nerd, and a nerd of many nerdisms, comics, scifi, movies, tv, trivia, etc. In this case it is the trivia that came into play you because I too knew of the worms long before reading that comic.   Scientists were doing experiments with intelligence and memory. To this end they put one of these worms in a maze with food at the end and observed as it got better and better at navigating the maze over time, it was learning and it remembered what it had learned. Now these worms have an interesting feature, like a star fish if you cut them up, they will regenerate into two or more worms. Both of these worms could navigate the maze.   Well they took this experiment a step further, they ground up one of these ‚Äúsmart‚Äù worms and fed it to a new worn and you know what? The worm who had not learned the maze before consuming the worm navigated the maze correctly in short order. Somehow the new worm had absorbed the memory or intelligence of the previous worm!   So if you add in DNA transfer along with intelligence/memory pass down in a world were mutant animals exist and space dinosaurs, I was totally there. It made perfect since to me and had a basis in actual science as well.

It was changed from Ninja to Hero in some European countries because of moral panic over ‚Äúvideo violence‚Äù.  They had already banned weapons like shurikens and nunchucks, and here comes a show with all of that stuff that‚Äôs directly targeting kids. They still talked about ninjas and ninjitsu all the time in the dialogue, it was only the name of the property that was changed.

as someone who grew up in the 80s/90s in the UK - the history is that censorship here was really high. The word "ninja' was deemed too violent by the board so they wanted to soften it by replacing it with 'hero', with violence in the show toned down or censored outright (which was awful as it was edited so badly that fights never made any sense & were nearly non-existent). Bruce Lee made nunchucks famous in the 70s but sadly gangs and thugs started to use them here (particularly football hooligans) so government outright banned them too, including all depiction of them on screen (all Bruce Lee and kung fu movies had scenes edited out including use of them in shows such as Thundercats and Hero Turtles, video games such as Soul Blade etc), Mikey was always shown to use his grappling hook instead. Thankfully the UK overturned this ban and censorship in the late 90s, TMHT was restored to TMNT now and all the shows and movies have everything restored. I've kept a lot of my VHS tapes of Thundercats and Hero Turtles as they're a part of history now haha

The Hero/Ninja thing wasn't to do with the word. In the UK around that time there was a lot of gang and youth crime involving Ninja weapons and knives and kids were buying butterfly knives and Nunchucks and using them to fight, so the word Ninja was dropped and Hero added.

Not sure about the rest of Europe but yes, they were known as the Teenage Mutant 'Hero' Turtles in the UK. Basically it was deemed that naming an ostensibly children's cartoon series, after a group of feudal Japanese assassins was inappropriate. Also Michelangelo lost his nunchucks as unlike staffs, swords and sais (who the hell even knew what a sai was in 1980/90s Britain?!) nunchucks are legally considered an offensive weapon and although you can buy and own them in the UK, you risk being arrested by the Police if caught with them in public with no valid reason to be in possession (eg you're a martial artist on your way to or from a training session). So again, considered inappropriate for a show aimed at children. As noted later films and seasons of the cartoon seemingly dispensed with the Turtles actually carrying or using any weapons, relying on strings of sausages or yoyos to defend themselves with. So problem solved I guess...?

On the Hero Turtles vs Ninja Turtles point - can't speak to elsewhere but here in the UK there was a massive moral panic about nunchucks after the release of Enter The Dragon. This lead to the BBFC recalling the film - Enter the Dragon was effectively banned here for ages and even when allowed out again it was heavily cut. The poster of the film is still different here. Anyway the head honcho at our film classification board waged a one man war against nunchucks for decades which is which Mikey never used his weapon and why ninja was taken out of the title

Real fact:  When the movie came out in Spain's theaters, "cowabunga!" was mistranslated to a curse word (the equivalent to "F** Great"!) and oftern made use of a few more F swearings. The movie was rated as PG-7, and most parents went shocked and concerned about bringing their 10 y.o. children to watch the movie. It was the BBC.  State-owned media, producing good quality stuff and syndicating popular things; overall very good service. But *very* politically-conscious. There had been some concern about the role in violence on TV viz youngsters, yada yada, and basically the top brass at the Beeb decided that the word 'Ninja' was unacceptable as it had violent connotations (nobody in the UK in the late 80s / early 90s actually knew what a ninja was; just assumed it was a killing machine warrior from the early 80s video nasties - so you can blame the VHS boom for this). So, the clueless BBC decided that a Saturday morning kid's show could not be about "ninjas" but "heroes" were appropriate, so that's what happened. It was the timid Beeb, fearing parental backlash and applying a really short best before date to get in there first and protect their asses / the tender psyche of the young viewer of course. We all used the word 'ninja' when we talked about it at school though.

The reason the Turtles were called "Hero Turtles" instead of Ninja Turtles in the UK was that the word Ninja was essentially banned from appearing in movie titles at the time, bizarrely. This followed the craze of ninja films in the 80s that Rich references. This was partly driven by the moral crusade against "video nasties" of Mary Whitehouse (who was also responsible for the word "Chainsaw" being similarly blacklisted) but her focus was more on gory horror movies.  The other, probably larger, reason ninjas were banned was that the craze surrounding the films led to a lot of people importing throwing stars and nunchucks. A friend of mine who must have been about 10 had a genuine, razer-sharp throwing star. He took it out and threw it at a telegraph pole, into which it embedded impressively. Unfortunately (or fortunately, really) a few throws later he missed the pole and it soared through a hedge, into a wheat field, and was never found.  Anything with nunchucks in was heavily cut by the BBFC, presumably to discourage the spate of idiots smashing themselves in the balls with the things. The Turtles films were cut in the UK because of this, and had much less nunchuck action. I remember at the time we just knew it had been cut, and this led to wild rumours about what the uncut American version contained. Someone I knew claimed to have seen it and painted a vivid picture of multiple beheadings and a turtles on April O'Neill gangbang.  Even The Secret of the Ooze was cut! The scene where Michaelangelo uses the sausages as weapons was cut because they looked too much like nunchucks. Arguably the only good thing to come out of the whole video nasty era was a note from the BBFC instructing the filmmakers to "Reduce to a minimum the dazzling display of swinging sausages" although now I think about it, it is possible they were referring to the scene with Splinter on the roof.

Great Re:view, a classic from my childhood. The thrill of being a mega turtles fan and seeing it come to the big screen was , well, like the thrill of being an adult as phase 1 MCU unfolded but better..  I'll never forget being in public school one day and the intercom going off asking me to come to the office. I go to the office and my mom is there signing me out for the day, she doesn't say anything until we get into the vehicle outside. Either really good or really bad I'm thinking at this point but she hands me a small parcel addressed to me. It was a VHS copy of the ninja turtles movie I had won. My mom took me home in the middle of the school day to make me pizza and let me watch this VHS I had won because she's a great mother and the 90s kinda rocked like that.  Now, to segway into movie info, that VHS was won as a child (89'/90') after dining in at a pizza hut lunch buffet. In 1990, the pizza wars were well under way and brutal. Little Ceasers was a powerhouse but actually the more expensive pizza back then. Domino's was a huge brand but still fighting for that delivery expert spot. Pizza hut was monolithic and the business one lunch buffet would do was enormous. You use to have to wait for a seat like a real restaurant.  Now it was actually pizza hut who had a deal with the movie for promotion, not Domino's. But the pizza wars were so nasty that PizzaHut opted for a Domino's delivery person to appear in the movie.  * The delivery driver pulls up on a rickety mo-ped. *The pizza boxes are bent up and tightly fastened with a stretchy cord on the back of the mo-ped in open air. *The pizza guy is LATE and can't find the address.  * He puts the pizza sideways through the grate- getting your pizza squished use to be a big deal. A huge no no. *The pizza guy is a little rude even though he's late.  *The pizza was made to look disgusting on purpose. If you watch movie , although Freddie finds it appealing, it looks nothing at all like the dominos pizza in the old commercial in this video. The cheese leaks down splinters head like blanc queso cheese and even as a kid it puzzled me until watching a documentary as an adult about the film, explained one of my biggest questions -- 'Why wouldn't you have your product looking delicious and your delivery guy having a fun experience with the turtles?' (Exactly what they would do in secrets of the ooze but sans pizza sponsor and if the first 5 mins of that film doesn't make you want pizza, then you don't like pizza). What we were watching was actually a pizza hut Psy Op for kids/viewers. Literally to make dominos service and product look terrible. Why risk putting a pizza hut pizza up there and risk half the audience thinking it looks gross when you could put the competitions pizza up there looking leaky and bland and getting money off your order cause they were late 'cause yea, that 30 mins or it's free stuff use to be a real thing. Genius marketing really. If you read this whole thing, you must be hungry, go grab slice.

21:33 I can only speak for germany, but it's kind of Cannon's fault. The Ninja Movies Cannon released in the late 70's/early 80's were infamous for their brutality over here and also sort of the first consideration of the word 'Ninja' in pop-culture here. Over the years everything containing the word Ninja was prejudged as being to brutal for audiences, especially children.

Teenage Mutant Ninja Turtles were redubbed as "Teenage Mutant *Hero* Turtles" in _some_ European countries due to some moral panicking about kids consuming ninja media in general (movies, games, comics, etc), though stuff like that would gradually let up some. I don't remember the weapons being censored in the cartoons I saw as a kid in Sweden, but they did do that typical thing where they only ever actually use the swords (or piercing/slashing attacks) against robots and stuff, then using more blunt force and grappling or dodging against living opponents.  My memory could be wrong though, this was absolutely ages ago.

The word "Ninja" was banned in the UK because of a complete panic around "video nasties"  Mostly cheap straight to video action and horror flicks which some people at the time believed were transforming children in Britain into complete psychopaths.  They decided to censor the word Ninja for fear kids would chop off each other's heads on the playground and replaced it by "hero" expecting it would have a positive influence on kids.

I actually saw TMNT III in theater.  I remember I was bored enough that my brother and I spent most of the movie talking instead of watching, and no one seemed to care.  The only part I really remember was the climax, when they somehow knocked the bad guy off the cliff into the ocean with an explosion, and...he just faded into a wave instead of splashing or anything.  It was ridiculously bad, and even as a kid I knew that was the result of the movie makers being extremely cheap and lazy.  The audience in the theater was silent for just a moment, and I heard another kid shout out loudly "That fuckin' sucked!"  The rest of the theater burst out laughing and there were a few comical boos at the movie.  No one watched the epilogue, and the theater started to empty.  My brother and I went home and talked about how that movie was a waste of our allowance to our mom the entire time.

The arcade game and TV programme had to be Hero Turtles however the film squeezed through with "Ninja" (due to being in the cinema and not broadcast to everyone) but received cuts due to the weapons. Regarding the "Ninja" vs "Hero" thing in Europe. The main issue was that studios would cater to the countries with the harshest regulations on the European market so that they only had to make one version instead of several for each country. So let's say for example that Germany would rate something as not allowed for children below the age of 7 and everyone else passed it as suitable for all children, they would still adjust it to make it suitable for all children in Germany and everyone would get that version.  But mostly I think it was moms overreacting as usual.  Although in the early 90's in Sweden where I grew up. We would practice roundhouse kicks on each other in school and make throwing stars and sais in shop class so they might not have been completely wrong...
Freddie and I have the same TMNT first-contact story, with the Palladium RPG book! I actually got an adventure module for the game before the core rulebook. As I understand it, it was a the British Board of Film Classification that wouldn't allow "Ninja" in the title. I don't know if it was the same for the rest of Europe.

The word "ninja" isn't forbidden for general use, but is considered unsuitable for a kids' cartoon and toys. That's why we also didn't get the Rambo cartoon over here.
As a European i can clarify the "Hero" part. This was only in the United Kingdom. Here in the Netherlands it was still the Teenage Mutant Ninja Turtles.  At the time, the British government was on the offensive against violence in children's television, and ninjas and nunchucks were both in the firing line. As such, in spite of the preexisting comic line, it soon became clear that Ninja Turtles wasn't going to be allowed near England's impressionable youth. Thus, the turtles needed to be heroes, not ninjas, and the cartoon theme song lyrics, action figure packaging, and video game box art needed to reflect that.  Since the movie wasn't being screened on children's television, it managed to escape the alterations and keep its original title

For the longest time I figured it was a false memory until I found out that was, in fact, a deleted scene. I went to the school Kindergarten Cop was filmed in while it was being filmed. I was a third grader and instead of being an extra, I opted out. While my classmates had to wait on buses for their scenes, I got to just wander around and see the movie get shot. It was awesome, even though I never got to meet Arnold Schwarzenegger, I did get to meet Ivan Reitman and lots of the actors. After Kindergarten Cop, Free Willy 1 & 2 were filmed in my neighborhood, a five minute walk away, and I got to be an extra in it, but my scene never got used. Astoria was awesome! You can't imagine being a kid in 1990 and finding out Arnold Schwarzenegger is going to film a movie in YOUR school.  One day, they announced over the intercom at school that Teenage Mutant Ninja Turtles III was going to be filmed in Astoria! This was right at the end of that being the hottest property for kids. It was like "wait, we got Schwarzenegger and now we're getting the Ninja Turtles too?" But, even though Ninja Turtles was filmed in the same area where Benji: The Hunted was filmed, about two miles from my home and you could go watch it be shot, which I did when I was six, you couldn't get anywhere near the set of Turtles 3. It was a really secretive shot. It wasn't even clear the costumes or actors would be there and it wasn't just B-Roll being shot. Then, there was one picture of the Ninja Turtles on the front page of the Daily Astorian, the local newspaper, and all the kids were heartbroken that the movie was being made out in the woods, completely closed off to the public. What a cruel trick it was to play on the local kids. An even crueler trick was how bad the movie was! It stunk, and it felt like karma because they didn't want anyone from the city anywhere near where the Turtles were.
For years I'd only even seen the first two or three minutes of this, because my mother thought we'd walked into the wrong movie and we couldn't convince her otherwise. We ended up watching Driving Miss Daisy instead.
The first movie I ever saw in theaters.
Turtles in Time was a huge part of my childhood, and the best thing about the German TV series of the time was the intro - I'm not sure I watched a lot of it, but I certainly remember that to this day ... super iconic.
The UK got really weird about certain martial arts stuff during the Bruce Lee and such martial arts craze. You'd get kids getting into fights with numb chucks. So of course the dumb ass knee jerk reaction was to ban a lot of seemingly random shit. Numb chucks were one, the word Ninja was another, and throwing stars. As a result you'd get a lot of games where they'd be replaced with kunai, and they'd toss those instead, and that kinda stuck around.

The whole Teenage Mutant Hero thing was because the Director of the BBFC (British Board of Film Classification) hated ninjas and nunchucks. Like, almost pathologically despised nunchucks to the point of having them edited out despite the presence of deadlier weapons in the same films,and insisting the Sausages were edited out of TMNT2 because they look like Nunchucks.

I'm from the UK, so I can weigh in on the Ninja/Hero thing. In the UK rather than the Satanic Panic and PMRC during the 80s we had a parallel hysteria over 'Video Nasties' and the 'Ninja Craze' causing children to become violent and dangerous. There was a reactionary backlash against the media becoming increasingly violent and having adult themes in general which was amplified by the Thatcher government and the press as a convenient scapegoat for lowering standards of living and to justify increasing the police presence throughout the country and the level of violence they were carrying out against the public.  It was effectively banned for any media that featured ninjas to be marketed towards children because any store that would have carried the toys or network that ran ran a program that prominently featured ninjas would have been targeted by pressure groups and any home video releases would have at minimum received a higher age certificate from the BBFC, if not being banned from sale outright due to the certificate being refused. Because of this the property was rebranded Teenage Mutant Hero Turtles and the theme song for the cartoon was dubbed to alter that one word. Certificates are much more strict over here, so it's illegal for anyone under the specified age to be provided a copy of any home video releases or a ticket for a film with a higher certification regardless of whether a parent or guardian accompanies them.

IIRC Just preceding the release of TMNT in the UK there had been a number of serious playground injuries (maybe even deaths - it was a long time ago) from kids copying WWE wrestling moves, and there was a whole cultural convention about violence in kid's media. In order to not be drawn in to that conversation, the decision was made to call them Hero rather than Ninja Turtles

Judith Hoag thought that Teenage Mutant Ninja Turtles was a horror film at first. She actually stated that she abandoned the April O'Neil role because the producers weren't treating the stuntmen on the set right as they were flown in from Hong Kong without Union protections and they would be instantly replaced as soon as they were injured.

I haven't watched this yet, but I'm so psyched for it. I recently watched TMNT with some people who've not seen it since childhood like myself, and some who never saw it, and while the humor is often silly and maybe a bit unappealing to me as an adult, the film itself is surprisingly beautiful in its craft, tone, pacing and direction. With fantastic fight scenes, great effects, a surprisingly prescient and lasting story, and moments that have stuck with me into adulthood. Strangely, of all the ninja films I've seen since, it's the one that felt the most like a mysterious force living among us, that vision of ninjas that were in popular culture.   It presented gangs in a sympathetic and somehow more realistic light than so many films around it, other than like, say, Suburbia. Where they are not a bunch of vile people, but generally alright people trying to find a purpose and life and community. And bizarrely still an important lesson about how to navigate life for many in the online space, as gangs are very real, just most have become somewhat abusive online micro-communities that similarly use and abuse people.  Also, the musical queues, the scenes, the lost sai, the recovery of their wounds in the country, Raphael's 'DAAAAAAAMN' they stick with me as deeply emotionally impactful than anything in any other film. It's a shame its sequel was so much less than this one.

The 80's were worse, when it came to protests about violence in cartoons. That was a time, when certain groups would have mock burials of Transformers & GI Joe toys, to protest the violent themes. Also, there were requirements that shows that might be potentially of interest to & viewed by kids were required to have a certain percentage of educational content. Cartoons AUTOMATICALLY fit this category. This was the reason that many cartoons had PSA's at the end, which Fensler made so popular - years later. I think, by the 90's,  those policies were on the way out.

The thing about the hero vs ninja is that back then and to an extent today, the USA are much more progressive about violence in media. In Europe and the UK or Australia we do not just discuss if something is bad, we keep up the old tradition of governmental censorship.  On the other hand we got more boobs, while this is seen slightly more puritanical in the US.  It is all smaller with the internet around, and there were ways around the law back then, but just look at Mortal Kombat or the many 80s style action movies.

As an european, I have to confirm that nija was changed to hero due to the fact (according to the censorship) it was "to violent to call it ninja." <--- remembering from the top of my head now as watching this video on this webzone. The word ninja and the meaning of it comes way way back from 11th to 14 century Japanese theatre called  Sarugaku (Monkey music) wich was a predecessor of modern day circus. As it grow/developed it become ninja - as in the martial art was theatrical. However the word ninja is a word for those whom mastered the martial art ninjitsu or master of an craft of field/activity - in this specific case; master of the craft of martial art.

The nunchuck issue in the UK was down to a do-gooder called Mary Whitehouse. She was the face of a censorship group who were responsible for the classifying of all the good movies from back in the day as Video Nasties. This led to a whole bunch of early horror being banned for quite a while, aswell as the removal of the use of nunchucks from a few pictures. Such as TMNT and Enter the Dragon.

Go Turtles
I wish I could take credit for the observation but I appreciated it: Someone noted all the turtles are given weapons antithetical to their personalities. Sais are basically really close-in defensive weapons, and they were given to Raphael, the most aggressive of the turtles. Michelangelo is the most carefree and lackadaisical, so he was given the nunchuks, which will hurt the user if they're not used carefully. Donatello is the tech nerd who loves complication, so he gets a wooden stick and made to work with simplicity. And Leonardo is a good guy and most moral/heroic of the bunch, so he was given swords to make him have to use lethal force because sometimes it's what you need.   I have no idea if that was intentional writing or not but it makes Splinter come out looking like a genius to make them all have to grow and develop their character and round them out. And ever since it was pointed out to me I notice it in Turtles media.

The whole space worm conversation reminded me of the worst part of the 2003 animated series, which is otherwise in my opinion the best of the four animated adaptations.  So, Shredder (who is an evil Utrom in this version) seemingly dies outside his robot suit in a fiery explosion at the TCRI building after 1.25 seasons adapting many of the original comic book stories.  The show passes it off as though he's really dead for a while, with a two parter where the various crime syndicates fight over the power vaccuum left by Shredder's death.  At the end of that episode, it's revealed that Shredder survived the explosion and was nursed back to health by tiny alien worms called "biocytes" that repaired his flesh.  It's easily the biggest asspull in the whole series, and ruins what could have been a solid send-off for the character.  Though, considering that Eastman finds the use of the worms in the comic funny now, and that he was heavily involved in the production of '03, I wouldn't be surprised if he brought them back in '03 as a joke.

Much of this is lost to time now, but in the early 2000s, Peter Laird was actually working with Steve Barron to create a 4-hour TMNT revival miniseries for the Hallmark Channel that would have served as a continuation to the original film.  It would have disregarded the second and third film entirely and returned Judith Hoag back as April O'Neil.   They spent somewhere around 2 years developing it before it was placed on indefinite hiatus and ultimately cancelled.   Supposedly, there was also a more complete cut of the original film with a few additional scenes (such as the turtles coming to the surface of a river) from the original trailer that has never been released and likely no longer exists.  As for the Beetleborgs mention, the turtles actually had their own Power Rangers spinoff series called "The Next Mutation" a few years after a planned fourth film never materialized.    Saban got the rights and produced the single season of the show for FoxKids, bringing in Kevin Eastman to help develop the series.  He introduced Venus, a long-lost 5th (female) turtle, much to the ire of fans and Peter Laird, who originally only had one rule that no female turtles be created.   The characters from Next Mutation crossed over in an early episode of Power Rangers in Space and used completely different voice actors than those from the actual series.  And although the Next Mutation series was actually the highest rated series on FoxKids, the production costs were too high to justify additional seasons.   When the Fox Family Channel premiered in 1998, one of the turtle characters randomly appears for about 5 seconds surfing across outer space in "Men in White," [AKA National Lampoon's Men in White] a Saban-produced made-for-TV movie (and future 'Best of the Worst' candidate) that was created to launch the network.   All that said, TMNT is the first movie I recall ever going to where the lines stretched well outside of the theater.  Also the first film I remember ever seeing twice in theaters.

In the UK the BBFC were very against so called 'video nasties' and refused to issue certificates for many films such as Driller Killer, Texas Chansaw Massacre, even Reservoir Dogs was unavailable at home for a while.  They had a bit of problem with martial arts movies and weapons, nunchuku in particular (Enter the Dragon was heavily cut) and ninjas were seen as a particular nasty sort.

So I'm not sure if this is 100% accurate but certainly seems to be in the right direction when it comes to the Ninja vs Hero thing.  In the 70s the large number of martial arts movies led the British Film Certification board to shite themselves and start listing specific weapons which shouldn't appear in films shown in the UK. The main spark for this was most likely Enter The Dragon (1973) which was instantly given an X rating for violence. Some newspapers at the time were shocked that it was even allowed an X rating rather than being outright banned.  Things got so out of hand that the 1988 Criminal Justice Act contained a list of banned weapons. Over half of the items on the list were ninja/martial arts weapons. Though that list has changed slightly over the years there are still several things on it directly relating to this time period. Footclaw, Handclaw, Hollow Kubotan, Shuriken, Kyoketsushoge, Kusarigama, and most hilariously of all the Kusari itself, which is simply weights attached to each end of a rope or chain, not too dissimilar from a bike chains with pair of padlocks.  The film side of this nonsense wouldn't end until the late 90s. That scene of Mikey playing with the sausages like nunchuks in TMNT 2? Cut from the UK release of the film. All scenes in the first film where nunchuks were visible were cut, edited or replaced.  The whole thing is a mess of politics and press madness some of which continues to this day in mindsets or left over laws.
The idea for the worms absorbing someone‚Äôs consciousness is actually  a swipe from the 1983 Alan Moore Swamp Thing story,  ‚ÄòThe Anatomy Lesson‚Äô.  Apparently planarian worms can learn to navigate a maze,  then get chopped up and fed to other planarian worms who then know the maze by absorption.

They're called "Hero Turtles" in the UK (and possibly Ireland?) because ninjas are thought of as cold blooded assassins there.  Trivia:  Ninjas are actually spies. The ninja thing being illegal was not a European thing, but a British one. It only happened there. In every other European country they were simply called (sometimes translated) Teenage Mutant Ninja Turtles. But back when the show came out, the UK was going through one of its more extreme 'moral outrage' phases (google 'Mary Whitehouse') and conservatives came up with all kinds of terms and concepts that were deemed detrimental to the developing mind. So in the UK only, they were for a time known as 'Teenage Mutant Hero Turtles', because the word 'ninja' was banned. This also explains why the nunchucks specifically weren't allowed. It was confusing here in The Netherlands, because we would sometimes see cartoons via the UK and sometimes via the US, and both names were used interchangeably without any explanation.

The change from Ninja to Hero was due to a fear of rising Japanese influence during their economic boon. Leo's swords are swords, Don uses a stick and Raph's sais could just be daggers. Mike's Nunchaku and the word ninja, however, were considered distinctively 'Japanese' and both needed to be scrubbed. Yes I know the sais and swords were Japanese design, but we're talking about xenophobic moms and idiot politicians.

The word "ninja" was problematic in the UK for a period of time in the eighties, even for IPs aimed at adults and would have made marketing the show unfeasible. That's why they renamed it to "hero" for all of Europe so that they wouldn't run into issues.

I know exactly what the dude was referring to about the worms... there was a study done which trained a bunch of worms to look for food near a lightbulb.  I think they weren't earthworms but like tiny little worms... but anyhow, they took the worms that had been trained to go toward the light, chopped them up into little pieces, then fed them to other worms.  And those worms started seeking the light.  Apparently that inspired a comic book.  Huh.  I was 11/12 in 1990, and absolutely loved Teenage Mutant Ninja Turtles.  The fact there was a comic book was known to me, but as a mythic thing which was R-rated and gory (at least how I heard it in elementary school... this was mostly pre-Internet, so rumors were big).  But I have a big memory attached to the VHS release... Afaik Ninjas was a big no no in Europe because adults were against the amount of violence in American kids shows. There was a big scare with stories going around about kids who accidently went blind because they played with homemade throwing stars. I think there were also stories of children accidently dying from playing with homemade "ninja weapons". So you simply couldn't sell a show/franchise aimed at kids with the word ninja in it.

The reason the first 5 episodes of TMNT (1987) were so well-animated was when it was on the drawing board, they figured 'okay, we'll pull out all the stops. Make five episodes, call it a pilot. If it gets renewed? Call it season one.' Those first five episodes were done by TOEI Animation would be why they look so good and hold up so well. After it was renewed the licensing deal was they had to have 63 episodes by the third season and they obviously couldn't afford TOEI Animation. That's why the quality takes such a dramatic hit from season 2 onward.  e: the iconic intro for the first few seasons was TOEI, too.

The change from Ninja to Hero applied mostly to the franchise‚Äôs name, since the characters don‚Äôt seem to have any issue using the term ninja. It is mainly due to Germany having the most draconian laws regarding entertainment aimed at children, which often resulted in censorship of some kind. For example, the Contra video game series had to be renamed Probotector and the main characters changed to androids due to the politically sensitive issue of the Iran Contras‚Äô fiasco. More often than not, the changes made to accommodate Germany would set the tone for European releases.

Rich's Sam's Club story.
I've always liked how the Turtles were characterized by the weapons they were given by Splinter.  Mike is the youngest and most erratic of the bunch and has to learn self discipline, so he gets a weapon that needs practice and focus so he doesn't hurt himself. Raph is emotional and aggressive, so he gets a short-range defensive weapon to keep his impulses in check. Don is the smartest, so he gets the simplest weapon, a stick, so he has to use his head to make it useful. And Leo is the leader of the group and gets the only weapon that is lethal by design, so must learn to carefully judge his actions.

Big Bad Beetleborgs is actually an Americanization of a series in the Metal Heroes franchise in Japan, which is in the same genre as Power Rangers. It's the same genre as Ultraman and Kamen Rider as well. But since Americans had no way or reason to know that in the mid 90's, we all just thought it was a Power Rangers ripoff.

The Hero Turtles thing was only over here in the UK. At the time anything that had Ninja, weapons or fighting and targeted at kids was banned as part of the ‚Äòvideo nasty‚Äô campaign after some kids brutally murdered another small child and it was blamed on horror and king fu movies. To get around it they called them hero turtles and despite the Turtles still fighting it got past the BBFC sensors without a question üòÇ

35:21 Rich Evans is right. The character of Splinter had been tortured and starved for weeks; plus, he is old (having been exposed to mutagen in his rat middle age). Regarding the British rename. In the 1980's the conservative British government manufactured a nonsense moral panic about ninja's and ninja weapons because someone got injured with ninja stars like one time? I don't even believe the initial incident was true but it doesn't really matter, government used it as a "won't someone think of the children" scheme to create a problem that didn't exist so they could provide a solution to make it seem like they were doing something. Point being, the name and weapons change was made to get it past British censors who were on a war path back then and would happily censor/ban anything if given half a chance.

I can explain why they retitled the show as "Teenage Mutant Hero Turtles" over in Europe. In Britain, the Tories (The Conservatives for any of ye across the pond) cracked down on violence in children's entertainment. So weapons like Nunchucks were removed as well as "Ninja" being replaced with "Hero".   Funny enough, the film kept the "Ninja" word in the title, but scenes were re-edited to cut down on Michelangelo using his signature weapons. Hell, for the sequel, they wanted Michelangelo swinging the sausage Nunchucks to be cut down. The BBFC (British Board of Film Classification) said this in their note:  _"After turtle takes down sausages and uses them as a flail. Reduce to minimum dazzling display of swinging sausages indistinguishable from chainsticks."_  - Some gobshite censor at the BBFC.  Come 2003, the new show just kept it as "Teenage Mutant Ninja Turtles", with no cuts or edits whatsoever. So what have we learned from these edits? Margeret Thatcher and her MPs should stay away from Parliament.

Big Bad Bettleborgs was produced by Saban, the same company as Power Rangers, presumably because they already had a relationship with Toei who produced both Sentai and Metal Heroes. So, it wasn't a ripoff so much as just a series made in the same trend. It's as much a Power Rangers ripoff as Power Rangers is an Ultraman ripoff.

17:37 he‚Äôs out of his damned mind. Having splinter be a mutated rat is way more consistent than him being a mutated human. The explanation for him knowing ninjitsu is that the mutagen enhances mental abilities as well, so when he was a pet he just watched his Master Yoshi. Then when he was exposed to the mutagen, he was able to access those memories. As kid in the 80s in Europe: Your parents won‚Äôt let you see violent stuff on TV in general. Exposing kids to graphic violence still is frowned upon - violence is the same as sex‚Ä¶ not a topic for kids. There was no GI Joe, Rambo or other US military type of cartoons on TV here. We got captain planet, Brave Star, He Man etc. and of course a lot of domestic stuff. I recall the TMNT being controversial because they featured ‚Äúactual‚Äù weapons, instead of fantasy versions of them. But generally kids toys were not supposed to wield weapons back jn the 80s. In the 90s and 00s I think Japanese shows brought in more violent themes via Dragon Ball Z and the various shows that followed here on after.

you know its funny how the censored version of the movie, that I watched as a kid, actually improves certain scenes, imo.  for example, the scene when splinter throws shredder off the roof, in the censored version splinter just sidesteps him and shredder falls off with no further dialouge, which in my eyes actually works better, because it looks like shredder just defeated himself through his blinding rage, and splinter was being calm and collected.  a great callback to the scene much earlier with raphael, when splinter tells him to not have his rage get the better of him.  and I think it works better because it doesnt look like splinter is consciously killing shredder, but really shredder is just killing himself because he's blinded by his rage.  also they cut out 1 or 2 of the worst jokes, so wasnt happy about hearing those when I watched the uncensored version, but sadly they kept the "claustrophobia" joke in there, which is pretty cringey, homophobia.

Weird fact: the german version is more kid friendly because they added way more quirky sound effects/micky mousing to all the action sequences which gives the whole movie a completely different feeling. They replaced every natural punching sound with some cartoon effect. Every single one.  It took me all the years to the age of DVD when I could watch the movie in english and realize that its a totally different tone.  Seeing them fight without doorbell ringing, coo coo clocks, cymbals or gong sounds for the first time was brilliant!  If you want a good comparison look up "Teenage Mutant Ninja Turtles (1990): Street Fight (German Audio)" here on youtube, you can find the english version easily aswell ( Teenage Mutant Ninja Turtles (1990) "Turtles vs Foot Clan" )

Glad the RPG game got brought up. When the cartoon first started I, as a child, was desperate for any Turtles related material I could get. I ran across the game and modules at my mall's Waldenbooks. Just flipping through the book I had no idea what an RPG even was, but seeing that imagery I just had to take the books home. I would recommend anyone into Turtles or RPGs to pick it up. Some things, like the image of the Terror Bears, have stuck with me for decades because the art is just that great. The game really opened up that world to me as a child and was my bridge to the collected graphic novels of the comics that were available then.   TMNT was a great introduction to other properties as well, like Usagi Yojimbo and Cerebus. Kevin Eastman was so very influential to comics outside of creating TMNT. Eastman took his money and started Tundra Press, which took huge chances on some wildly inventive books that would have never been printed otherwise, not to mention the direct grants he gave to many creators for years until the money started drying up. The man literally "squandered" the majority of the early TMNT fortune he made on trying to help other creators make their dreams come to life. That is the most respectable thing I could imagine. Eastman wasn't satisfied rolling around in his collected wealth, he tried to raise the whole industry up through supporting the most important figures in comics, the writers and artists.

Jay is spot-on about the post-production sound effects and music. There was a push by the studio before release to lighten the tone a bit. Can't remember where I heard it, but I clearly remember the story.  Also, the change to make them "hero" turtles in the UK was limited to the cartoon. The movie was still Ninja turtles, but it was rated 12, because it was deemed too violent for young kids.
